        };

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202417809')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="65726267f40a48fe9fa1374bc6afbe8a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0bc45c42-f5a6-468f-94fc-fa2565ea7857'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

title="ePDF" class="coolBar__ctrl pdf-download" role="menuitem"><img src="/specs/products/acropolis/pericles/releasedAssets/images/pdf-icon-169a2eb30e52100e76dfa5f4b66998e6.png" alt="">PDF</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="coolBar__section coolBar--sections"><a id="sections_Ctrl" href="javascript:void(0)" data-db-target-for="sections" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="sections_Pop" role="button" class="coolBar__ctrl"><i class="icon-list-unordered"></i><span>Sections</span></a><div data-db-target-of="sections"    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d5601356" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium (Na) metal is regarded as an ideal anode for sodium metal batteries (SMBs) due to its high theoretical specific capacity and low electrochemical potential. However, its utilization is still restricted by the notorious dendrites and unstable solid electrolyte interface (SEI). Herein, a zinc nanoparticle decorated 2D diamond (diamane) modulated commercial polypropylene (Zn-diamane/PP) separator is dedicated to mitigating these issues for the longevity of sodium metal anodes. Interestingly, the sodiophilic Zn-diamane/PP separator significantly accelerates the sodium ion mobilization and promotes a uniform flux, thus effectively preventing dendrite growth and enhancing the stability of the SEI layer. A Na||Na symmetric cell with Zn-diamane/PP separator demonstrates a prolonged lifespan exceeding 5000 h at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Notably, it sustains stability for &gt;1000&nbsp;h at an ultra-high current density at 25 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The dendrite-free deposition morphology and the formation of an inorganic-rich inner SEI layer have been investigated through comprehensive characterizations and theoretical calculations analysis. Furthermore, a full cell comprising a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C cathode, Zn-diamane/PP separator, and Na metal anode demonstrate a high reversible capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 500 cycles. These results demonstrate the potential of Zn-diamane/PP separator for long-cycle lifespan SMBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202417809-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202417809-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The high abundance and economic viability of the raw materials of sodium have pushed sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) to a critical research focus.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0001_R_d5601348e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0006_R_d5601348e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium (Na) metal, featuring a high theoretical capacity (1166 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and low electrochemical potential (−2.714 V vs standard hydrogen electrode), has become an ideal anode material for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0007_R_d5601348e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Energy">7</a>-<a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0013_R_d5601348e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Powder Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, there are some critical issues such as dendrite growth, high electrochemical reactivity, and large volume expansion, which have seriously delayed the commercial application of sodium metal anodes (SMAs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0014_R_d5601348e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Energy">14</a>-<a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0017_R_d5601348e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To tackle these issues, researchers have proposed diverse strategies, including 3D sodiophilic host design,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0018_R_d5601348e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Energy">18</a>-<a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0021_R_d5601348e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Appl. Phys. Rev.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> artificial solid-electrolyte interface (SEI),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0022_R_d5601348e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0028_R_d5601348e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> electrolyte and additive optimization,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0029_R_d5601348e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Energy">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0030_R_d5601348e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nanoscale">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and solid-state electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0031_R_d5601348e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0035_R_d5601348e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, separator modulation has emerged as an economically feasible and integrative strategy for enhancing the performance of SMAs, thus sparking extensive investigative efforts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0036_R_d5601348e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a>-<a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0040_R_d5601348e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Materials">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Currently, the predominant membranes utilized in commercial lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are polyethylene (PE) and polypropylene (PP).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0041_R_d5601348e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these membranes are not suitable for SMBs with the drawbacks of low Young's modulus,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0042_R_d5601348e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nat. Energy">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> slow thermal diffusion ability,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0043_R_d5601348e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Membr. Sci.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> inferior electrolyte wettability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0044_R_d5601348e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nat. Energy">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To date, a variety of modification strategies have been developed to address these limitations, such as solid-state NASICON-type fast ionic conductor layer coating onto the PP membranes (NZSP@PP),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0045_R_d5601348e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sodiophilic biomacromolecules modified glass fiber membrane (AF@GF),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0046_R_d5601348e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and structural s-2D mesoporous functional polymer heterostructure membranes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0047_R_d5601348e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nat. Commun.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the long cycling and rate performance of SMA is greatly improved by these pioneer works, the complicated fabrication process and material selectivity are still great challenges for the practical application of SMAs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0041_R_d5601348e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is imperative to select the functional materials to modulate the separator with a simple process to manipulate the ion transportation and deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0048_R_d5601348e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Diamane is a newly emerged 2D diamond with ultra-high Young's modulus and high thermal diffusion ability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0049_R_d5601348e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nano Lett.">49</a>, <a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0050_R_d5601348e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Diam. Relat. Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, we reported NH<sub>2</sub>-functionalized diamane coated on the commercial PP separators to regulate the Na ion diffusion and enhance the deposition kinetics. Owing to the sodiophilic NH<sub>2</sub>-diamane and macroporous structure, the Na||PP/NH<sub>2</sub>-diamane||Na cell exhibits a sustained stability for &gt;3200 h at 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0051_R_d5601348e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Eng. J.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the amino functionalization of diamane is still subjected to the complicated hydrolysis and thermolysis process. Therefore, it is necessary to develop another sodiophilic material onto the diamane surface to tune more sodiophilic to regulate the Na ion deposition chemistry.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, sodiophilic zinc (Zn) nanoparticles (NPs) were decorated on the diamane/PP separator to fabricate the Zn-diamane/PP separators to efficiently regulate the Na ion flux, guide the Na deposition and form a stable SEI layer for longevity SMA (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0001">1</a>). The designed Zn-diamane was selected for its remarkable ionic conductivity, high mechanical strength, high thermal stability, and excellent sodiophilic character.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0052_R_d5601348e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Nano Energy">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zn was selected as an ideal sodiophilic material due to the merits of high abundance, low cost, and a low nucleation overpotential of 6.2 mV at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, which is the smallest among the measured cheap metals (Zn, Ti, Co, Cu, Ni, and Fe) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The low nucleation overpotential is due to the electrochemically in-situ formed Na-Zn alloy on the diamane surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0053_R_d5601348e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The formed Na-Zn alloy exhibits an exceptional binding affinity with Na ions and a low diffusion barrier for Na nucleation and propagation. Consequently, the elaborated Na-Zn alloy enhances the Na deposition chemistry, not only can uniform Na ion flux inhibit the “hot spots” on the anode surface and enhance the Na ion transference number, but also guides the homogeneous Na nucleation and endow controlled reversal Na metal growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0054_R_d5601348e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the volume expansion of the Na-Zn alloy is as small as 16.7%, which contributes to the long-cycle stability of Na metal anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0055_R_d5601348e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the designed Zn-diamane/PP separator plays a pivotal role in the establishment of a stable robust SEI layer on the surface of Na metals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0056_R_d5601348e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Energy Environ. Sci.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the Zn-diamane/PP separator owns much-improved electrolyte wettability, thereby significantly enhancing Na ion diffusion kinetics and reducing concentration polarization. With the above merits, the Na||Na symmetric cell employing Zn-diamane/PP separator could stably cycle for &gt;5000 h at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Even at a tough condition of 25 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the cell could still sustain over 1000 h. Finally, when assembled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (NVP@C) as a cathode, the full battery with Zn-diamane/PP separator can still maintain a high reversible capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 500 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, thus making Zn-diamane/PP separator very promising for realizing high-energy density SMBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202417809-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2efba4bb-7e3e-4bd7-bb09-2dcaf060469e/adfm202417809-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2efba4bb-7e3e-4bd7-bb09-2dcaf060469e/adfm202417809-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/370e9ee0-ea5e-4d0d-8ca7-ad02abfc8487/adfm202417809-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2efba4bb-7e3e-4bd7-bb09-2dcaf060469e/adfm202417809-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202417809-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202417809" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the Na ion deposition behaviors using (left) a conventional PP separator-induced dendrite formation, and (right) a Zn-diamane/PP separator-induced dendrite-free morphology.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202417809-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202417809-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The fabrication procedure of the Zn-diamane/PP separator is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). First, micro-sized diamonds were synthesized by a commercial high-temperature high-pressure (HTHP) process. Subsequently, diamane nanoflakes were mechanically exfoliated from the synthesized diamonds using a ball milling process, followed by a series of acid treatment and rising processes to isolate the diamane nanoflakes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0057_R_d5601348e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Int. J. Refract. Met. H.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The resulting diamane nanoflakes exhibit a typical 2D morphology with lateral dimensions of ≈200 nm and a thickness of ≈4.2 nm (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The crystal structure of the diamane was confirmed by the X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). There are three prominent peaks located at 43.9°, 75.3°, and 91.5° corresponding to the (111), (220), and (311) crystal planes of diamond (JCPDS card No. 06–0675).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0058_R_d5601348e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Light Sci. Appl.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Second, the prepared diamane nanoflakes were coated on the surface of the PP separator by a conventional blade-coating method. The PP separator is composed of irregular macropores with pores ranging of 50–800&nbsp;nm.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0054_R_d5601348e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the diamane nanoflakes coating process, the diamane/PP separator turns from white to dark grey and exhibits a uniformly flat surface composed of the diamane nanoflakes and nanosized interparticle nanopores (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Third, Zn NPs were uniformly deposited on the surface of diamane/PP separator by a magnetron sputtering process, yielding a Zn-diamane/PP separator. After sputtering deposition, Zn NPs are homogeneously deposited onto the surface to form Zn-diamane/PP separator, maintaining a flat and uniform morphology with a dark-colored surface (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2a</a>). The overall thickness of the fabricated Zn-diamane/PP separator is ≈37 µm, consisting of a 25 µm PP layer, an 11 µm diamane layer, and a 1 µm Zn layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2b</a>). The evidence of the Zn NPs uniformly distributed on the surface of diamane/PP separator with a thickness of ≈1 µm is also demonstrated from the EDS elemental mapping images (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). Enlarged transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images show that the Zn NPs with an average size of 5 nm are homogeneously distributed on the surface of diamane (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2c</a>). The insert in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2c</a> shows the lattice spacing of 0.25 nm corresponding to the (002) crystalline face of the Zn NPs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0059_R_d5601348e741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv. Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From XRD pattern of Zn-diamane/PP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2d</a>), in addition to the peaks of 43.9° and 75.3° belonging to diamane, the peaks located at 36.3°, 39.0°, 43.2°, 54.3° and 70.1° can be attributed to (002), (100), (101), (102) and (103) crystal planes of Zn metal (JCPDS card No. 04–0831).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0060_R_d5601348e749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - ACS Energy Lett.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectrum shows a single dominant peak at 1321.8 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which is attributed to the characteristic peak of diamond, indicating the absence of the peaks associated with graphene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0051_R_d5601348e760" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Eng. J.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The functional groups formed on the surface of the diamane nanoflakes generated during the multiple acid treatment process are measured by the Fourier transform infrared spectrometer (FTIR) analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2f</a>). The peaks at 3441.4, 1631.0 and 1103.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be attributed to the O─H bond related to the asymmetric stretching vibrations of methylene.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0061_R_d5601348e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Diam. Relat. Mater.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 1794, 1400, and 1254–1005 cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to the C═O, C─O, and ─COOH functional groups, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0050_R_d5601348e778" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Diam. Relat. Mater.">50</a>, <a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0061_R_d5601348e778" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Diam. Relat. Mater.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202417809-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cb0197e0-0e79-44d0-a215-d3f7d9049893/adfm202417809-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cb0197e0-0e79-44d0-a215-d3f7d9049893/adfm202417809-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a7f11c61-ccca-4a73-ad8c-c3c039135c25/adfm202417809-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cb0197e0-0e79-44d0-a215-d3f7d9049893/adfm202417809-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202417809-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202417809" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of Zn-diamane/PP separator. a) Top-view and b) cross-sectional SEM images of Zn-diamane/PP separator. Inset in a) is a photograph of the Zn-diamane/PP separator used in CR2032. c) TEM image of the Zn NPs decorated on the surface of a diamane nanoflake. Inset in c) is an enlarged HRTEM image showing the crystal facial distances of diamane and Zn NPs. d) XRD of PP, diamane/PP and Zn-diamane/PP separators. e) Raman and f) FTIR spectrum of the diamane. g) Thermal diffusion ability and h) Young's modulus of Zn-diamane/PP separator. i) Contact angle measurement of the electrolyte on Zn-diamane/PP separator.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Fast thermal conductivity and elevated thermal stability are critical for the separator to inhibit the thermal-induced dendrites generation and ensure the safety of batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0062_R_d5601348e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Adv. Energy Mater.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zn-diamane/PP separator exhibits increased thermal diffusion ability and improved thermal stability. To further validate the improved thermal diffusion properties resulting from the diamane coating, a temperature distribution experiment was conducted with a laser heated at a fixed point for 10 s. The results show that the highest temperature of PP separator is 87.5&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information). However, the highest temperature of the diamane/PP is 64.8&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information), and it can be further reduced to 58.1&nbsp;°C for Zn-diamane/PP separators (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2g</a>). This result indicates that the Zn-diamane/PP separator exhibits a superior thermal diffusion rate compared to that of the diamane/PP and PP separators. In addition, Zn-diamane/PP separator also exhibits improved thermal stability. For example, the PP separator is curved at 90&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, Zn-diamane/PP and diamane/PP separators still maintain the original shape even at 150&nbsp;°C. Moreover, Zn-diamane/PP separator exhibits a similar Young's modulus (5.61 GPa) as diamane/PP separators (5.34 GPa), which is almost one order higher than that of the PP separator (0.51 GPa) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). It is reported that Young's modulus of the SEI layer should be &gt;3.5 GPa to inhibit the Na dendrites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0063_R_d5601348e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - ACS Energy Lett.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high Young's modulus of the separator can effectively prevent Na dendrites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0064_R_d5601348e834" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Adv. Mater.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Young's modulus of our designed Zn-diamane/PP separator is superior to the reported separators used in the Na metal anodes, such as exterior-nonporous separator (SPF) (1.2 GPa), cobalt-based metal–organic framework nanowires (Co-NWS) separator (0.12 GPa), and CNC-based nanocomposite nanofibrous separators (S-3500) (0.18 GPa) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0036_R_d5601348e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0041_R_d5601348e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0065_R_d5601348e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Mater.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the fabricated Zn-diamane/PP separator not only shows high mechanical strength, but also exhibits foldable, flexible, and twistable properties (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Moreover, the Zn-diamane/PP separator also shows improved electrolyte wettability as evidenced by contact angle measurement. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0002">2i</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information), the contact angle of the electrolyte and Zn-diamane/PP is merely 17.21°, significantly lower than that of the pristine PP separator (41.66°) and diamane/PP separator (18.41°). In addition to the reduced contact angle, the Zn-diamane/PP separator also exhibits the most rapid electrolyte diffusion rate, which is fully covered by the electrolyte within 10 s as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information). These findings suggest that the Na ions are transported rapidly and uniformly across the surface of the Zn-diamane NPs. The improved electrolyte wettability would significantly bolster Na ion diffusion speed and enhance the surface kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0066_R_d5601348e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - ACS Applied Energy Mater">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To demonstrate the modulation effect of Zn-diamane/PP separators on the reversibility of Na metal deposition, symmetric Na||Na cells with different separators (PP, diamane/PP, Zn/PP, and Zn-diamane/PP separator) were evaluated at various current densities. As the current density gradually increased from 0.5 to 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0003">3a,b</a>), the symmetric cell with Zn-diamane/PP separator still shows the flattest plating/stripping curves and the lowest overpotentials (from 11.7, 21.3, 37.1, 68.8 to 75.6 mV) compared with cells with other separators (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The flat Na deposition/stripping plateau suggests that the Na ions rapidly diffuse at the surface of the modulated separator and form a stable SEI layer with the assistance of the Zn-diamane layer. To demonstrate the role of the reversible deposition induced by the Zn-diamane/PP separator in improving the cycle stability, the symmetric cell with Zn-diamane/PP separator was evaluated at various current densities with different capacities. For example, at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the cell with PP separator exhibits an unstable deposition and stripping curve and the largest polarization with a hysteresis voltage of 59.4 mV at 150 h, and short circuits after 200 h of operation due to the formation and growth of the dendrites as well as the unstable SEI layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0003">3c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0067_R_d5601348e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">67</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cell with the diamane/PP separator shows a low polarization with a hysteresis voltage of 36.4 mV at 200 h, and much better cycle stability with a more flat plating/stripping curve until 1100 h. However, the plating/stripping curve of the cell with diamane/PP separator becomes unstable from 1700 h, the hysteresis voltage is increased to 96.7 mV until 1900 h, and then dramatically increased until 2000 h. The cell with Zn/PP separator only can stably cycle for 500 h (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the cell with Zn-diamane/PP separator exhibits the most stable performance, lowest polarization voltage, and longest lifetime up to 5000 h. It shows a flat deposition/stripping curve, and maintains a polarization voltage of 29.6–53.6 mV even during 4000 h of operation, slightly increasing to 66.6 mV until 5000 h (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0003">3c</a>). The improved cycle lifetime and the reduced polarization voltage can be attributed to the synergistic effect of sodiophilic diamane nanoflakes and Zn metal NPs, which guide uniform Na deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0051_R_d5601348e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Eng. J.">51</a>, <a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0068_R_d5601348e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Adv. Mater.">68</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon increasing the current density to 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a high areal capacity of 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the cell with Zn-diamane/PP separator can stably sustain &gt;1000 h, which is much higher than that with PP separator (200 h) and diamane/PP separator (700 h) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0003">3d</a>). Even at an ultra-high current density of 25 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the cells with Zn-diamane/PP separator can also stably operate for &gt;1000 h (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The superior long-cycle stability even at high current densities and low hysteresis voltage can be ascribed to the effective rectification effect of Na ion promoted by the Zn-diamane coated onto the PP separators. To underscore the benefits of the Zn-diamane/PP separator, an overview of the extended cycle performance of various separators explored for Na metal anodes is presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0003">3e</a>. The comparative analysis shows that our designed Zn-diamane/PP separator exhibits superior long-cycle stability at mild measurement conditions and maintains its long-cycle stability even at an elevated current density at 25 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202417809-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f21ddd2d-f96a-402a-b981-ec8a59e25ba4/adfm202417809-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f21ddd2d-f96a-402a-b981-ec8a59e25ba4/adfm202417809-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2b2a9719-2bfd-4953-9502-446f6a1b2c27/adfm202417809-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f21ddd2d-f96a-402a-b981-ec8a59e25ba4/adfm202417809-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202417809-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202417809" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the Na||Na symmetric cells with PP, diamane/PP, and Zn-diamane/PP separators. a) Rate performance at various current densities ranging from 0.5 to 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, and b) corresponding voltage hysteresis. Voltage–time profiles at c) 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and d) 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Insets in c) are the magnified voltage profiles of Na||Na symmetric cells with PP, diamane/PP and Zn-diamane/PP separators at ≈80, ≈1000, ≈2000, and ≈4000 h, respectively. e) The comparison of the electrochemical performance of Na||Na cells with Zn-diamane/PP and the representative separators. f) Current response curves of the cells with different separators at a polarization voltage of 10 mV. g) Ionic conductivity, and h) histogram of ionic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> transference number of different separators.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The diffusion kinetic behavior of the Na ions on the electrodes affected by the prepared separators is explored by investigating the exchange current density and charge transfer resistance. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), Zn-diamane/PP separator exhibits a higher exchange current density of 3.46 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> than that of the PP (0.71 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>) and diamane/PP separator (1.54 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>), indicating the fastest electrode reaction kinetics on the Na anodes with the assistance of Zn-diamane/PP separator.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0069_R_d5601348e977" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Nanoscale">69</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements revealed that the initial charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) of the cell with Zn-diamane/PP separator is 65.2 Ω, which is significantly lower than that of the PP separator (193.4 Ω) and diamane/PP separator (73.6 Ω) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). After 10 cycles, the R<sub>ct</sub> of the cell with Zn-diamane/PP separator is reduced to 6.1 Ω, remaining substantially lower than that of the cell with PP separator (74.9 Ω) and diamane/PP separator (12.7 Ω) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). These results indicate that the Zn-diamane/PP separators play a critical role in improving the kinetic interface between the electrode and the electrolyte, including enhancing the exchange current density and reducing the charge transfer resistance.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The dedicated separators also affect the Na ion transference number and Na ionic conductivity, which are calculated via the Vincent-Bruce method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0070_R_d5601348e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">70</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417809-fig-0003">3f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information), the Na ion transference number for the Zn-diamane/PP separator is as high as 0.66, which is much higher than that of diamane/PP separator (0.60) and PP separator (0.45). According to the dendrites nucleation Sand's time formula:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417809-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417809-bib-0071_R_d5601348e1014" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 71 - Energy Environ. Sci.">71</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202417809-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d0022c63-9c7f-4f5c-9bee-a802e0bdcbf6/adfm202417809-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202417809:adfm202417809-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202417809-math-0001.png">
